<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10164482787050132619"><strong>The third installment in Jasper Fforde’s <em>New York Times </em>bestselling series follows literary detective Thursday Next on another adventure in her alternate reality of literature-obsessed England</strong><br /><br />Jasper Fforde has done it again in this genre-bending blend of crime fiction, fantasy, and top-drawer literary entertainment. After two rollicking <em>New York Times</em> bestselling adventure</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10164482787050132619"><strong>The third installment in Jasper Fforde’s <em>New York Times </em>bestselling series follows literary detective Thursday Next on another adventure in her alternate reality of literature-obsessed England</strong><br /><br />Jasper Fforde has done it again in this genre-bending blend of crime fiction, fantasy, and top-drawer literary entertainment. After two rollicking <em>New York Times</em> bestselling adventures through Western literature, resourceful BookWorld literary detective Thursday Next definitely needs some downtime. And what better place for a respite than in the hidden depths of the Well of Lost Plots, where all unpublished books reside? But peace and quiet remain elusive for Thursday, who soon discovers that the Well is a veritable linguistic free-for-all, where grammasites run rampant, plot devices are hawked on the black market, and lousy books—like the one she has taken up residence in—are scrapped for salvage. To make matters worse, a murderer is stalking the personnel of Jurisfiction and it’s up to Thursday to save the day. A brilliant feat of literary showmanship filled with wit, fantasy, and effervescent originality, this Ffordian tour de force will appeal to fans of Douglas Adams and P. G. Wodehouse. Thursday’s zany investigations continue with <em>Something Rotten</em>. Look for the five other bestselling Thursday Next novels, including <em>One of Our Thursdays is Missing</em> and Jasper Fforde’s latest bestseller, <em>The Woman Who Died A Lot</em>. Visit jasperfforde.com for a ffull window into the Ffordian world!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10164482787050132619">...more</a>

      </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_17071889">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17071889" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/220791-lena" class="left imgcol" title="Lena"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442474289p2/220791.jpg" alt="Lena" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/17071889?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 17, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/220791-lena" class="user" title="Lena">Lena</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/220791-lena?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer17071889">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1285198987803640533">I’m sad to say I didn’t enjoy this book anywhere near as much as the first two in the series. While I think Fforde’s choice to set the action almost entirely in the Bookworld was an intriguing one, I also got the sense he was in over his head. <br /><br />Like many of the partially completed books in the Well of Lost Plots, there is a great amount of creativity on display here, but also a lot of half-baked ideas and poorly developed characters. The action took place in so many different settings and with su</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1285198987803640533">I’m sad to say I didn’t enjoy this book anywhere near as much as the first two in the series. While I think Fforde’s choice to set the action almost entirely in the Bookworld was an intriguing one, I also got the sense he was in over his head. <br /><br />Like many of the partially completed books in the Well of Lost Plots, there is a great amount of creativity on display here, but also a lot of half-baked ideas and poorly developed characters. The action took place in so many different settings and with such a huge cast of characters that I never felt entirely grounded within either the story or the people in it. The book just seemed to be going in too many different directions at one time, and while a good chunk of the tale was resolved by the ending, there were enough loose ends left lying about I couldn’t tell what had been set up for the next book and which he had just sort of forgotten about. <br /><br />That said, I did still enjoy the copious literary references, the smart humor, and Fforde’s unusual way of musing about the process of creating books. But the experience was unsatisfying enough I’m not sure I’ll continue. Die-hard Next fans: does the fourth one get any better?<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1285198987803640533">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_30326909">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30326909" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1413439-stephen" class="left imgcol" title="Stephen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" alt="Stephen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/30326909?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 03, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1413439-stephen" class="user" title="Stephen">Stephen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=science-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">science-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=audiobook" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=humor-and-satire" class="actionLinkLite">humor-and-satire</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=fforde-pickup" class="actionLinkLite">fforde-pickup</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=2000-2005" class="actionLinkLite">2000-2005</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=books-about-books" class="actionLinkLite">books-about-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=love-those-words" class="actionLinkLite">love-those-words</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=comfort-food" class="actionLinkLite">comfort-food</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer30326909">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9337458353142351491">5.0 to 5.5 stars. I liked this book so much that when I finished it I had to really wonder whether I should go back and re-read the first two books in the series (which I have 4 stars and 3 stars respectively). The writing was absolutely superb, the plot was engaging and very original and the literary references hysterical. I found myself more than once jumping to Wikipedia to find out from which book a particular character or reference originated. <br /><br />A few fun examples (1) a rage counseling sessio</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9337458353142351491">5.0 to 5.5 stars. I liked this book so much that when I finished it I had to really wonder whether I should go back and re-read the first two books in the series (which I have 4 stars and 3 stars respectively). The writing was absolutely superb, the plot was engaging and very original and the literary references hysterical. I found myself more than once jumping to Wikipedia to find out from which book a particular character or reference originated. <br /><br />A few fun examples (1) a rage counseling session for the characters of <a title="Wuthering Heights by Emily Brontë" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8150640.Wuthering_Heights">Wuthering Heights</a>, (2) Miss Havisham from <a title="Great Expectations by Charles Dickens" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2619.Great_Expectations">Great Expectations</a> in a contest with Mr Toad (from <a title="The Wind in the Willows (Signet Classics) by Kenneth Grahame" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49954.The_Wind_in_the_Willows__Signet_Classics_">The Wind in the Willows</a>) to set the land speed record and (3) King Solomon as head of the Jurisfiction's Arbitration Department. <br /><br />In a world of cookie cutter fantasy and science fiction, Jasper Fforde has created something that is truly original. HIGHEST POSSIBLE RECOMMENDATION!!! </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9337458353142351491">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span id="like_container_review_30326909" class="likeItContainer"><a href="/rating/voters/30326909?resource_type=Review" rel="nofollow" id="like_count_review_30326909"><span class="likesCount">16 likes</span></a> · <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F27001.The_Well_of_Lost_Plots" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" rel="nofollow">Like</a></span> · <a href="/review/show/30326909?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1">see review</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_124371572">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124371572" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1309606-f-r" class="left imgcol" title="F.R."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1378152880p2/1309606.jpg" alt="F.R." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/124371572?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 03, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1309606-f-r" class="user" title="F.R.">F.R.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/107379.The_Well_of_Lost_Plots" title="Paperback 9780340825938" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer124371572">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8421627828403487491">In which my irritation at this series reaches a boiling point.<br /><br />I had an odd reaction to the two previous Thursday Next novels, a curious mixture of subtle enjoyment and distinct annoyance. The enjoyment came from the fact that I’m a man who loves books and they were distinctly literary reads. But there was also a huge amount of quirkiness (never a quality I particularly like) and an arch ‘oh-isn’t-this-<em>soooo</em>-clever!’ self-satisfaction to the proceedings. There were points in the previous books wh</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8421627828403487491">In which my irritation at this series reaches a boiling point.<br /><br />I had an odd reaction to the two previous Thursday Next novels, a curious mixture of subtle enjoyment and distinct annoyance. The enjoyment came from the fact that I’m a man who loves books and they were distinctly literary reads. But there was also a huge amount of quirkiness (never a quality I particularly like) and an arch ‘oh-isn’t-this-<em>soooo</em>-clever!’ self-satisfaction to the proceedings. There were points in the previous books where I laughed, but also points when I wanted to hurl my copy hard into the nearest brick wall. It’s rare that a series can walk such a fine tightrope, but ‘The Well of Lost Souls’ marks the point where I – at least – fell off.<br /><br />The previous books split Thursday Night’s adventures between the real world (which is in fact a fantasy version of our world) and the world of books – where she can interact with great characters from fiction. The ‘real world’ stuff contained most of the plot, while the stuff set in books worked as amusing little sketches. In this volume however it’s all set in the book world, so its sketch after sketch, knowing reference after knowing reference, beloved character behaving oddly after beloved character behaving oddly. There is no grounding, nothing to reign in the silliness – it’s just wackiness built on wackiness until I really wanted to scream. <br /><br />One of the most irritating things is how lazy it all seems. You have to hand Fforde bouquets for his inventiveness, but the clever jokes and ideas he comes up with are never developed into anything else. So we have Mr Toad in an automobile race against Miss Haversham; rage counselling sessions in ‘Wuthering Heights’; and ‘Macbeth’ retold for Yeast. All very silly stuff which doesn’t really go any further than those brief descriptions I’ve just given you. There are numerous instances of sketches revealing their points in the first few lines, but staying on for page after page and hammering those points to death. Probably the nadir is a retelling of the trial scene from ‘Alice in Wonderland’, which lacks all of Carroll’s true invention and adds absolutely zero to the original. (After awhile it reminded me of those little comic asides in the cartoon series ‘Family Guy ‘ – a show I remain ambivalent to. They are pointless, silly and not even half as funny as they would like to be.) <br /><br />And yet within this superficial silliness, Fforde actually asks us to care. But how can one really care when the Miss Haversham of this book finds herself in jeopardy? All we have to do is open ‘Great Expectations’ and there she is again. And the Miss Haversham of this novel isn’t so well developed as a character <em>away from</em> Dickens that a reader would come to care for Fforde’s version instead. She – and many other literary figures – are just tossed in as throwaway jokes, but so little else is done with them that they remain pencil sketches of other author’s characters.<br /><br />Though perhaps the most irritating thing of all is that throughout this book there seems to be a smug belief that this one of the cleverest and funniest novels anyone could ever read. The witty allusions, the literary knowledge and the endless, endless puns – you can almost hear the author slapping his back in congratulations at his own brilliance. Unfortunately this reader just found the whole thing incredibly tedious, and so even though the series goes on from here, I have now disembarked. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8421627828403487491">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_926197196">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/926197196" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/25353295-carmen" class="left imgcol" title="Carmen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1412617445p2/25353295.jpg" alt="Carmen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/926197196?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 28, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/25353295-carmen" class="user" title="Carmen">Carmen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/77022.The_Well_of_Lost_Plots" title="Hardcover 9780670032891" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Literature lovers, people who like funny books</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Carmen by: </span>
      Library
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=traditionally-published" class="actionLinkLite">traditionally-published</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer926197196">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6324098710425946625">3rd BOOK IN THE "THURSDAY NEXT" SERIES. THIS REVIEW CONTAINS SPOILERS FOR THE PREVIOUS TWO BOOKS. YOU HAVE BEEN WARNED. NO SPOILERS FOR THIS VOLUME (#3)<br /><br />Thursday Next is a war veteran. She has traveled into books. She has worked for Special Ops. She has fought a Supreme Evil Being. Her skills and smarts are legendary.<br /><br />Thursday Next is pregnant. The father, her husband Landon, is dead - eradicated by those Goliath Corporation bastards. They traveled back in time and killed him as a 2-year-old. It's</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6324098710425946625">3rd BOOK IN THE "THURSDAY NEXT" SERIES. THIS REVIEW CONTAINS SPOILERS FOR THE PREVIOUS TWO BOOKS. YOU HAVE BEEN WARNED. NO SPOILERS FOR THIS VOLUME (#3)<br /><br />Thursday Next is a war veteran. She has traveled into books. She has worked for Special Ops. She has fought a Supreme Evil Being. Her skills and smarts are legendary.<br /><br />Thursday Next is pregnant. The father, her husband Landon, is dead - eradicated by those Goliath Corporation bastards. They traveled back in time and killed him as a 2-year-old. It's only by sheer luck that Thursday still has their (her's and Landon's) child inside her.<br /><br />Where can she go? Both Special Ops and Goliath are hunting her down. Goliath wants to keep her locked up and run experiments on her, to find out how she can travel in and out of books and how they can exploit that to take over the world. There's only one place where she can be safe from them - inside a book.<br /><br />So Thursday sets up house in a third-rate unpublished detective novel, trying to stay safe through her pregnancy. She misses her husband fiercely. She gets a job working as a Apprentice Jurisfiction Agent. <br /><br />Jurisfiction polices books. They fix misspellings, keep characters from rebelling, help settle strikers, kill Grammasites, etc. etc. Thursday's beloved mentor, introduced in the previous novels, Miss Havisham (from Great Expectations) is helping her become a full agent.<br /><br />Other prominent characters that might seem familiar - Falstaff, Cheshire Cat, Minotaur, Marianne Dashwood, Heathcliff, the Red Queen, and Count Dracula. There are also a bunch of characters from unpublished novels, which is basically Fforde playing off tropes.<br /><br />Fforde has more fun than seems possible. He provides an onslaught of puns and literature jokes. These books are tailor-made to people who love books, reading, and literature.<br /><br />Thursday Next is an AMAZING female character. She is human. Fforde never lingers on her looks, sex appeal, or outfits. She gets sh*t done. She is smart, decisive, quick-thinking, witty, and knows how to use a gun. In this novel she's pregnant - and also running around saving the (book) world. She is very capable and strong-willed. She's great - and not because Fforde is TELLING us she's great, it's just in every action and word of hers. Great job of showing, not telling, Fforde. A great example of a good female character written well by a male author...it CAN be done.<br /><br />I highly recommend this book to people who love literature. Fair warning - if you haven't read the classics (Austen, Poe, Shakespeare, Bronte, Carroll, Stoker, etc. etc.) you will not get some of the jokes. That's the only reason that this didn't get five stars from me. You really have to be well-versed in classic literature to get the full power of what Fforde is doing here. But it's obvious that Fforde is extremely intelligent with an amazing sense of humor.<br /><br />This series, in my opinion, is better than HITCHHIKER'S GUIDE because it is just as funny, but also has high stakes and makes you feel for the characters.<br /><br />P.S. There is a great, subtle Stephen King reference on page 79 that cracked me up.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6324098710425946625">...more</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer202248911">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1436203147711173214">I was surprised to see how many people didn't like this one as much as the first two. Personally, I loved it. Interestingly though, before I launched into the Thursday next books, I had already read the 1st 2 of Fforde's ' Nursery Crimes’ series, which intersects loosely with the book world setting of this 3rd novel, and for me a large part of the reading pleasure here was in the cleverness of that intersection, And so perhaps that has some impact upon the way I read the book.<br /><br />Like the previous 2</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1436203147711173214">I was surprised to see how many people didn't like this one as much as the first two. Personally, I loved it. Interestingly though, before I launched into the Thursday next books, I had already read the 1st 2 of Fforde's ' Nursery Crimes’ series, which intersects loosely with the book world setting of this 3rd novel, and for me a large part of the reading pleasure here was in the cleverness of that intersection, And so perhaps that has some impact upon the way I read the book.<br /><br />Like the previous 2 in the series, The Well Of Lost Plots is, if nothing else, a light-hearted romp (and yes, I do apologise for the cliche,) in this one Jasper Fforde is clearly having fun with, and exploring fully, the potential of both the characters and world he's created. For the reader with a keen eye for wordplay, passing interest in science, literature, and crime writing, and a healthy sense of the absurd, this book ticks all the boxes.<br /><br />Or, at least, it did for me.</span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/83445-lisa-vegan" class="user" title="Lisa Vegan">Lisa Vegan</a>

        rated it

           <span class="uitext reviewText">almost anyone who enjoys humor, mysteries, sci-fi, fantasy, the English language, and books</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer17637796">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10738305774437747553">I slogged through most of the first fifth or so of this book; I preferred Thursday in her “real” world of the first two books. I was concerned that this third book wouldn’t be as enjoyable as I’d expected it would be. I ended up loving it though, and laughed as much as I did while reading the first two books, and cared as much about Thursday and certain other characters as much as well.<br /><br />This book was kind of all over the place more than the first two books in the series, but there were so many wo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10738305774437747553">I slogged through most of the first fifth or so of this book; I preferred Thursday in her “real” world of the first two books. I was concerned that this third book wouldn’t be as enjoyable as I’d expected it would be. I ended up loving it though, and laughed as much as I did while reading the first two books, and cared as much about Thursday and certain other characters as much as well.<br /><br />This book was kind of all over the place more than the first two books in the series, but there were so many wonderful tidbits. One of my favorite was the explanation for the difference between the American vs. British spelling of words ending in or and our, such as labor vs. labour. <br /><br />I was distressed by the elimination of one character, but assume I’ll have many others to enjoy in the remaining books in the series.<br /><br />I’m one of those readers that reads absolutely every word on &amp; in a book, and this is one book where some of the “extras” are part of the book and part of the fun.<br /><br />I’m really enjoying this Thursday Next series by Jasper Fforde. He’s a genius.<br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_73120824">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73120824" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/642041-sarah" class="left imgcol" title="Sarah"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442653226p2/642041.jpg" alt="Sarah" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/73120824?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 07, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/642041-sarah" class="user" title="Sarah">Sarah</a>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer73120824">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6174573235938187275">There's something in me that wants to dislike these books, but I just can't. They are both absurd and ridiculously clever, and stuffed full of jokes that only a gramma(rfan) could love. I appreciate that Fforde explodes the formulae of every genre he skewers, refusing to return his characters to the starting block for the next book. I sometimes find everything a little too clever and self-congratulatory, and the quotes that start the chapters irritate me with their look-ma-no-exposition expositi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6174573235938187275">There's something in me that wants to dislike these books, but I just can't. They are both absurd and ridiculously clever, and stuffed full of jokes that only a gramma(rfan) could love. I appreciate that Fforde explodes the formulae of every genre he skewers, refusing to return his characters to the starting block for the next book. I sometimes find everything a little too clever and self-congratulatory, and the quotes that start the chapters irritate me with their look-ma-no-exposition exposition, but some tiny detail usually comes along to redeem the book again. <br />Fun stuff. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_27712214">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27712214" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2543454-honor" class="left imgcol" title="Honor"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Honor" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/27712214?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 19, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2543454-honor" class="user" title="Honor">Honor</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Writers and other book nerds.</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer27712214">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17678604647387760707">I seem somewhat doomed to find series via a book somewhere in the middle. I'm sure it happens to everyone, but it -feels- like it happens to me a bit more often.<br /><br />So... Except for people who find it accidentally, who'd read a review of this book? Likely someone who's interested in perhaps reading it... Who, I'd guess, would be someone who's already read the first two. So, this review's probably useless. None the less....<br /><br />This book (as I'm sure is true for the rest of the series) is meant as somethi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17678604647387760707">I seem somewhat doomed to find series via a book somewhere in the middle. I'm sure it happens to everyone, but it -feels- like it happens to me a bit more often.<br /><br />So... Except for people who find it accidentally, who'd read a review of this book? Likely someone who's interested in perhaps reading it... Who, I'd guess, would be someone who's already read the first two. So, this review's probably useless. None the less....<br /><br />This book (as I'm sure is true for the rest of the series) is meant as something of a reader's book... Not casual, "I read two or three novels a year" readers... But Readers. If I could italicize or use bold face here, I would. By extension, since I imagine so many decent writers are Readers, it's also meant to be a Writer's book.<br /><br />It's filled with inside jokes about plot development, scene construction, dialog conventions, exposition, character development, and so on.<br /><br />Since it also takes place in "the book world" - the alternate dimension that fictional characters inhabit both while working and while "off duty" - there is also -loads- of business, name dropping, and inside jokery about other books.<br /><br />The problem with this is that... Well... Have you ever been playing around with a group of friends who all saw the same film or read the same book as yourself, and all felt somewhat similar about it, and you go off on this big long tear about the possible interaction of the characters and what might have been and how -hilarious- that might have been? And, since you were in that group, and high on laughter, in that special way that laughter has of making everything else seem a little more funny... By the end of it, you're all in tears, laughing at just about -anything- but... If you showed it to a stranger, or could even view it yourself a month later without the benefit of having been there, it might not be nearly so compelling.<br /><br />So... I felt that a lot of the references to other books were a bit... Overdone. The quick references in passing, I enjoyed very much. a few lines with Rudyard Kipling's Painted Jaguar in the elevator... Quite nice. Bits of conversation between Shakespeare's Beatrice and Benedick were simply brilliant in the authenticity they carried over. But, for me, the anger management session with all the characters from Wuthering Heights, working through their seething hatred of Heathcliffe just seemed to drag on, and on, and on...<br /><br />All told, I'm happy I read it, and I will read the others when the opportunity arises... I just wish the author would keep the inside jokes quick and to the point.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17678604647387760707">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_5211008">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5211008" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/174013-anna" class="left imgcol" title="Anna"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1193060601p2/174013.jpg" alt="Anna" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/5211008?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 10, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/174013-anna" class="user" title="Anna">Anna</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Outlanders with humor</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/174013-anna?shelf=i-recommend" class="actionLinkLite">i-recommend</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/174013-anna?shelf=magical-mystical-worlds" class="actionLinkLite">magical-mystical-worlds</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/174013-anna?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/174013-anna?shelf=fun-ny" class="actionLinkLite">fun-ny</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer5211008">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6816476257843028514">In this third book, Thursday Next goes for a small holiday in a poorly written and unpublished novel in the Well of Lost Plots within the Book World. While there, she ends up joining the Book World police (Jurisfiction) as an apprentice to the Dickens <em>Great Expectations</em> character Mrs Haversham.<br /><br />As in the previous two novels, this one if full of funny dialogues and meetings with characters from some of the great classics (Dickens, Brontë and Verne are just a few). <br /><br />The books really revolves around</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6816476257843028514">In this third book, Thursday Next goes for a small holiday in a poorly written and unpublished novel in the Well of Lost Plots within the Book World. While there, she ends up joining the Book World police (Jurisfiction) as an apprentice to the Dickens <em>Great Expectations</em> character Mrs Haversham.<br /><br />As in the previous two novels, this one if full of funny dialogues and meetings with characters from some of the great classics (Dickens, Brontë and Verne are just a few). <br /><br />The books really revolves around the making of literature; whereas Outlanders (ie we in the real world) think that authors are the creators of books, all in the Book World know that without the Generic Characters (who can be shaped into any personality type a plot would need), the Backstory Workshop, the holesmiths who fix holes in narratives, grammaticists who chase down grammacites and mispeling vyruses, Rage Control Meetings for the characters to prevent books such as Wuthering Heights from imploding, there would be no books.<br />Add to this the Villains and Monsters (including a Minotaur that runs amok and a quite sentimental Count Dracula) and you understand the need of the Book World police (Jurisfiction).<br /><br />All in all the book is very entertaining for all its details and originality. However, the details are sometimes at the expense of the plot, which was something that book 1 and 2 suffered from too. I still highly recommend the series.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6816476257843028514">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_27028832">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27028832" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/123293-angela" class="left imgcol" title="Angela"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1215874509p2/123293.jpg" alt="Angela" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/27028832?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 22, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/123293-angela" class="user" title="Angela">Angela</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer27028832">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3505428612085240672">Absolutely amazing. I'm convinced that Fforde is one of the most brilliant authors writing fiction today.<br /><br /><em>The Well of Lost Plots</em>, rather than being more of the same from the world of Thursday Next and Jurisfiction, is something fresh, still original, never boring, and simply... fabulous. I adore this series for so many reasons... not the least of which being that it, much like <a title="Harry Potter" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Harry%20Potter">Harry Potter</a> or <a title="Abarat" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Abarat">Abarat</a>, is too often viewed as a children's book when it (and the others) are really nothing of the sort.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3505428612085240672">Absolutely amazing. I'm convinced that Fforde is one of the most brilliant authors writing fiction today.<br /><br /><em>The Well of Lost Plots</em>, rather than being more of the same from the world of Thursday Next and Jurisfiction, is something fresh, still original, never boring, and simply... fabulous. I adore this series for so many reasons... not the least of which being that it, much like <a title="Harry Potter" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Harry%20Potter">Harry Potter</a> or <a title="Abarat" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Abarat">Abarat</a>, is too often viewed as a children's book when it (and the others) are really nothing of the sort.<br /><br /><em>The Well</em> had plenty of violence and sadness scattered throughout. Sad moments in a fictional book about fictional characters are quite strange, somehow. Strange, but not bad.<br /><br />In this volume, the idea of the <a title="Nursery Crimes" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Nursery%20Crimes">Nursery Crimes</a> series is introduced, and I can't wait to give them a whirl, too. I expect just as much joy from that series as from Thursday Next and her pseudo-autobiography.<br /><br />Fforde has become a very important member of my beloved authors list... possibly even right up there beside <a title="Stephen King" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Stephen%20King">Stephen King</a>... and that's saying a lot.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3505428612085240672">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3802605430259442816">This series is really becoming more and more delightful. For example, a word storm is sweeping through Bookworld and threatens Drama.<br /><br /><em>"It could tear a hole straight through 'The Scarlett Letter' at that rate," exploded Bradshaw, "and litter the whole book with dramatic events!"<br /><br />The consequence of this was terrible to consider - a new version of 'The Scarlet Letter' where things actually happen.</em> Snerk.<br /><br />You'll be glad to know that the <em>thirty-two English students at Frobisher High School in Michigan</em> who were in the process of studying it were successfully distracted by someone pulling the fire alarm, and those reading it for pleasure assumed that they had been momentarily distracted and had lost their place, so that the book was quickly put back to rights with no reader being the wiser.       <a href="/user/show/381149-martine" class="user" title="Martine">Martine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1117722.The_Well_of_Lost_Plots" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780340830611" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=postmodern" class="actionLinkLite">postmodern</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=modern-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">modern-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=humour" class="actionLinkLite">humour</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=british" class="actionLinkLite">british</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer12502938">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7379174033854286880">In the third instalment of the series, real life becomes a bit too dangerous for Thursday, so she goes into hiding in the book world. And not just any part of the book world, but the Well of Lost Plots, where unpublished novels languish. Here, while the pernicious Aornis Hades tries to erase her memories, Thursday continues her training to become a Jurisfiction agent. Which is not as easy as it might sound, for characters are failing to show up for their Rage Control Meetings, murderous Minotaur</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7379174033854286880">In the third instalment of the series, real life becomes a bit too dangerous for Thursday, so she goes into hiding in the book world. And not just any part of the book world, but the Well of Lost Plots, where unpublished novels languish. Here, while the pernicious Aornis Hades tries to erase her memories, Thursday continues her training to become a Jurisfiction agent. Which is not as easy as it might sound, for characters are failing to show up for their Rage Control Meetings, murderous Minotaurs, verbivores, grammasites and mispeling vyruses are wreaking havoc, and if that weren't spectacular enough, the revolutionary new book-operating system UltraWord is about to be launched, which may not be such a good thing. In short, Thursday's dreams of an easy, tranquil pregnancy are quickly dashed, never to return.<br /><br />Once again, Fforde's imagination knows no bounds. He pokes fun at everything from Verne, the Bronte sisters and Lewis Carroll to Microsoft and generic thrillers, and has a splendid time parodying bad fiction and good fiction alike. Sadly, however, he spends so much time world-building and losing himself in interesting ideas that the plot suffers. Very little actually happens in the first three fourths of the book; mostly it's an overly long description of life in the book world. The humour is a bit off, too -- while there are plenty of half-smiles, there are few genuine laughs, like the ones in the previous two books. Still, the book sets the stage for Book 4, and as such is a useful addition to the series.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7379174033854286880">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer10915386">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11949481355886011994">In this third installment of the Thursday Next literary mystery series, our agent finds herself working for Jurisfiction in the Book World, residing in one of the unpublished novels to be found in the Well of Lost Plots. Fforde creates a clever fantasy world and uses numerous literary puns, which make The Well of Lost Plots worth reading. The story itself, however, is not really gripping, and Thursday's character has never seemed well developed or "real" to me. I keep reading this series because</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11949481355886011994">In this third installment of the Thursday Next literary mystery series, our agent finds herself working for Jurisfiction in the Book World, residing in one of the unpublished novels to be found in the Well of Lost Plots. Fforde creates a clever fantasy world and uses numerous literary puns, which make The Well of Lost Plots worth reading. The story itself, however, is not really gripping, and Thursday's character has never seemed well developed or "real" to me. I keep reading this series because of the flashes of humour and the pure pleasure of being "in" on the literary jokes. The storytelling itself, however, leaves something to be desired. </span>

      <a href="/user/show/10278283-neil" class="user" title="Neil">Neil</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <a href="/book/show/6563333-the-well-of-lost-plots" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1424014716">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14280113392936445442">This was the 3rd book in the Thursday Next series, and the most confusing so far. The original 2 books were mainly set in the real world with the occasional foray into the Book World. This time around Thursday seeks sanctuary from the Goliath Corporation, by hiding out in an unpublished book held in the Well Of Lost Plots. <br />Whilst there she is carrying on as Miss Havisham's apprentice, as she endeavours to become a full Jurisfiction agent. As if she didn't have enough problems she must also battl</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14280113392936445442">This was the 3rd book in the Thursday Next series, and the most confusing so far. The original 2 books were mainly set in the real world with the occasional foray into the Book World. This time around Thursday seeks sanctuary from the Goliath Corporation, by hiding out in an unpublished book held in the Well Of Lost Plots. <br />Whilst there she is carrying on as Miss Havisham's apprentice, as she endeavours to become a full Jurisfiction agent. As if she didn't have enough problems she must also battle with a mindworm released by Aornis Hades, which is trying to erode her memory. Then there are missing Jurisfiction agents, and also those who have died in suspicious circumstances.<br />Personally I didn't enjoy this book as much as the other two. Perhaps there were simply too many sub-plots littered throughout, some were concluded but others were not. The Book World was a wonderfully intriguing idea, but some of the way it was handled made me feel as though I were back at school again. Some of the characters from other works of literature were used well, but some were almost a little too clever. There was a nice link up to another series by the writer though.<br />Hard bitten Fforde fans will enjoy this book, but it could well turn aside those who pick this book up by chance.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14280113392936445442">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9820678185324554972">I really enjoy the premise of this series, but this book (#3) did not hold my attention as did the previous 2 installments of the story. I found it very easy to lay aside this story for long stretches of time while I read other things. That, to me, is not a good sign. I eventually kind of made myself finish it.<br /><br />I'm not ready to give up on this series yet, however. For one thing, I want to know what happens to the main character -- or, to be more precise, how the major plot elements set up in book #2 are resolved. This part #3 was inventive and parts of it did entertain me (so, 3 stars), but it didn't move along the main character's development much at all from the earlier books. Some other reviewers have commented likewise.<br /><br />So, all in all, there was just enough here to keep me signed on for the next book in the series, but I feel this book was not as engaging as the others I have read thus far. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11308187812313226733">I found the first half rather tedious and thought I'd like this book considerably less than the first two Thursday Next books: the BookWorld is introduced at length and it seems like an endless stream of literary in-jokes. But around the middle it really picks up and turns into a very suspenseful and hilarious thriller. Just the showdown I did not find very convincing, just like that of «Lost in a Good Book»: perhaps the «evil new technology» trope was used a little too bluntly.<br /><br />Here's a great be</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11308187812313226733">I found the first half rather tedious and thought I'd like this book considerably less than the first two Thursday Next books: the BookWorld is introduced at length and it seems like an endless stream of literary in-jokes. But around the middle it really picks up and turns into a very suspenseful and hilarious thriller. Just the showdown I did not find very convincing, just like that of «Lost in a Good Book»: perhaps the «evil new technology» trope was used a little too bluntly.<br /><br />Here's a great behind-the-scenes by the author: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.jasperfforde.com/sfhub.php" target="_blank">http://www.jasperfforde.com/sfhub.php</a><br /><br />Very entertaining to read about Fforde's struggle with copyright in order to get permission to use others' characters. About the Minotaur that had to replace a Morlock: "Dangerous, fond of human flesh, imprisoned, in the public domain. He had all the necessary criteria." On the other hand: "Oddly, I didn't need permission to use Godot because he isn't a character."<br /><br />Fforde admits that if he had his way, there would be a lot more aircraft geekery in his books. But as it is, I find the scene where Thursday and Randolph start up the Bristol Pegasus Aero-engine while discussing Randolph's feelings for Lola particularly brilliant. All this painstakingly detailed tech talk is so different from the rest of the book, completely unexpected and makes for a very nice contrast with the dialogue it's interleaved with.</span>

  <a href="/user/show/4728718-algernon" class="left imgcol" title="Algernon"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451037240p2/4728718.jpg" alt="Algernon" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/274243108?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 15, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4728718-algernon" class="user" title="Algernon">Algernon</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4728718-algernon?shelf=2012" class="actionLinkLite">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer274243108">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3519582772440885472">The series is starting to lose its appeal for me. It is still very fun and smart, in fact this third book may be the boldest, most imaginative Thursday Next book so far. But I feel the author gets distracted too easily from the main plot and is more concerned with sidequests and making witty plays on words and dropping names than with character development or pacing.<br /><br /><em> Made wholly on location within the Well of Lost Plots. </em><br />This is both the main attraction and the main problem of the book for me. T</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3519582772440885472">The series is starting to lose its appeal for me. It is still very fun and smart, in fact this third book may be the boldest, most imaginative Thursday Next book so far. But I feel the author gets distracted too easily from the main plot and is more concerned with sidequests and making witty plays on words and dropping names than with character development or pacing.<br /><br /><em> Made wholly on location within the Well of Lost Plots. </em><br />This is both the main attraction and the main problem of the book for me. Thursaday next has retreated into the Book World were abstract concepts roam free and where you can interract with every character ever written. The possibilities for mischief and for fanciful twists on known tropes are endless, and perhaps I can understand how the author got caught up in his own creation. But I wonder if the book would hold the same appeal to the casual reader who is less familiar with the hundreds of books used for inspiration here.<br /><br />Some scenes are hillarious in their echo of literary conversations I've had on internet forums - like the Bookworld parliament where they hold endless debate on the different literary genres and whether a book belongs to SF or to fantasy or to horror categories, or on the different flavors of detective novels.<br /><br />The patient reader is rewarded with a well constructed finale, bringing the apparently random previous encounters into a logical and spectacular movie style finale. I am planning to continue with the series, but not as a top priority.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10708239320258142858">I absolutely loved this book just like the first two in the series. It was a little harder to get into this one but that's because I had to read the actual book rather than listen to the audiobook. (The version I got from the library was corrupted.) The Thursday Next books do not lend themselves well to skimming. There's just so much that you miss. That's why the audiobook version is so great, at least one of the reasons why.<br /><br />Once I realized this and slowed down, I enjoyed this reading as much as</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10708239320258142858">I absolutely loved this book just like the first two in the series. It was a little harder to get into this one but that's because I had to read the actual book rather than listen to the audiobook. (The version I got from the library was corrupted.) The Thursday Next books do not lend themselves well to skimming. There's just so much that you miss. That's why the audiobook version is so great, at least one of the reasons why.<br /><br />Once I realized this and slowed down, I enjoyed this reading as much as the audiobook version. In fact, I'm pretty sure the section with the myspelling vyrus would not have come across quite as well when read aloud as it did in the printed word.<br /><br />While I missed Thursday's alternate reality "real" world, I enjoyed the complete Book World setting and all the allusions to major works of literature. (I'm also pleased that I have a passing familiarity with most of the books referenced. These references are a book nerd's heaven.)<br /><br />I can't wait to start listening to the next (no pun intended) book in the series. I also must repeat my strong recommendation that all bibliophiles read these books. They're fantastic.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10708239320258142858">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer694899919">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6651841911968361047">Every time I read another Thursday Next book I figure that's the last one I'm going to bother with. Not because they're bad - they're actually rather charming - but because there are so many literary references that I feel I'm not really appreciating them as much as I could be. And I don't want to bother with the prerequisite reading to catch up. Anyway, this is the third book in the series, and Thursday has settled in an unpublished novel for the duration of her pregnancy. At the same time, she</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6651841911968361047">Every time I read another Thursday Next book I figure that's the last one I'm going to bother with. Not because they're bad - they're actually rather charming - but because there are so many literary references that I feel I'm not really appreciating them as much as I could be. And I don't want to bother with the prerequisite reading to catch up. Anyway, this is the third book in the series, and Thursday has settled in an unpublished novel for the duration of her pregnancy. At the same time, she is training to become a Jurisfiction agent, dealing with the memories of her eradicated husband being erased, and raising two young generics trying to figure out what kind of characters they will become. She is visited by her grandmother (and it just occurred to me that it was never fully explained just how old Granny managed to travel into the book world), deals with footnote spam, and attempts to solve the murder of several of her fellow agents. The whole thing is actually quite a lot of fun, and there were points when I laughed out loud at the absurdity of it all. I don't know that I'll necessarily continue the series, but I won't rule it out either.<br /><br />I would like to note that the unpublished novel in which Thursday stays is no longer unpublished - the story eventually became <a rel="nofollow" href="http://melydia.zoiks.org/2010/04/the-big-over-easy/">The Big Over Easy</a>. I actually think my having read that book first made this one more enjoyable, since I knew the nursery rhyme characters would sooner or later be infiltrating the generic detective story. It was also fun to see the plainer origins of the often zany characters from that series.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2916940370185936551">I want to be a Jurisfiction Agent. I have no idea what degree you have to get to be on this career path, but I'm going to find out. Jasper Fforde does not disappoint with this novel. It is fun and fully immerses us in the book world. Emily Gray does a great job with the narration.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText2916940370185936551">I want to be a Jurisfiction Agent. I have no idea what degree you have to get to be on this career path, but I'm going to find out. Jasper Fforde does not disappoint with this novel. It is fun and fully immerses us in the book world. Emily Gray does a great job with the narration. She has a diverse set of voices and believe me with the number of characters in this book this was no easy task.<br /><br />Our Ms. Next becomes a Jurisfiction agent in the Well of Lost Plots. This is a purely fictional world filled with Ms. Havisham, The Cheshire Cat, Heathcliff, etc. You also have a vast number of characters from books that have not been published and generics. You'll have to read the book to truely understand how enjoyable the generics are. Ms. Next is still trying to get Landon back and is in hiding - that is why she is in the Well of Lost Plots.<br /><br />Enjoy this book. It is science fiction but focuses on a many literary concepts such as the idea that there are no more original thoughts/plots and characters are just recycled versions of other characters so you can hire on multiple Heathcliffs, Merlins, and so on. It is delicious for any book lover.<br /><br />Emily Gray, as mentioned above did a great job. I bring attention to this because I was critical of her performance in the previous Next Novel. She did not handle male characters well previously. She overcame this issue. Landon was a miniscule part of this book but there were many male characters and she did a fabulous job. If you are on the fence because the narration of the last book left you disappointed don't worry. I believe you will be pleasantly surprised. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_85342702">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/85342702" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/824216-melissa-rudder" class="left imgcol" title="Melissa Rudder"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1201213962p2/824216.jpg" alt="Melissa Rudder" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/85342702?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 07, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/824216-melissa-rudder" class="user" title="Melissa Rudder">Melissa Rudder</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer85342702">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11833406562033512660">Jasper Fforde's <em>The Well of Lost Plots</em> might be the best <em>Thursday Next</em> book so far, especially showcasing all that I've come to appreciate in Fforde's novels: his imaginative alternate realities, literary knowledge, clever word play, and suspenseful plot development. From witty phrasings to its compelling story, <em>The Well of Lost Plots</em> was simply delightful.<br /><br />While Fforde's third novel has all of the literary gags of its predecessors--for instance, literary characters collecting for Jurisfiction me</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11833406562033512660">Jasper Fforde's <em>The Well of Lost Plots</em> might be the best <em>Thursday Next</em> book so far, especially showcasing all that I've come to appreciate in Fforde's novels: his imaginative alternate realities, literary knowledge, clever word play, and suspenseful plot development. From witty phrasings to its compelling story, <em>The Well of Lost Plots</em> was simply delightful.<br /><br />While Fforde's third novel has all of the literary gags of its predecessors--for instance, literary characters collecting for Jurisfiction meetings keep fruitlessly waiting for Godot before they can get started--Fforde's work goes beyond eliciting snickers from bibliophiles and really begins to create a conceptual mythology for the literary world, explaining how book are created or, for instance, how Uriah Heep became such a bastard and the last chapter of <em>Ulysses</em> became so... unintelligible. The best part is that his work is so enveloping that I can see myself, a few years from now, beginning to explain to an inquisitive class reading <em>A Tale of Two Cities</em> that Americans spell "colour" without the "u" because of a huge "u" shortage a few decades ago, and then realizing, <em>Shoot, I'm mixing up fiction and reality again. Oops.</em><br /><br />I must admit, three books into the series, that, in one significant way, Fforde's plots have always disappointed me. Each novel introduces a variety of conflicts and it is always my hope that, by the end, each separate strand of conflict and mystery would weave together to form a beautiful tapestry of suspense and resolution, Dickens style. But instead, the last three to four chapters tend to lead the reader through a series of climaxes and resolutions all connected only in that our heroine is their focus and agent, until the reader reaches an exhausted denouement. <em>The Well of Lost Plots</em> has this same problem, yet, somehow, it wasn't as frustrating. I cared about each separate conflict and, even though the end of the world wasn't looming as it was in the second novel, I still felt like this novel had the most at stake. I believed in its urgency.<br /><br />As I expected, <em>The Well of Lost Plots</em> was a delicious piece of literary candy. Not too nourishing, but worthwhile nonetheless.<br /><br />"Books may <em>look</em> like nothing more complex than words on a page, but they are actually an infinitely complex imaginotransference technology that translates odd, inky squiggles into pictures inside your head."<br /><br />"After all, reading is arguably a far more creative and imaginative process than writing; when the reader creates emotion in their head, or the colors of the sky during the setting sun, or the smell of a warm summer's breeze on their face, they should reserve as much praise for themselves as they do for the writer--perhaps more."<br /><br />"<em>Anything</em> devised by man has bureaucracy, corruption and error hardwired at inception."<br /><br />"Failure concentrates the mind wonderfully. If you don't make mistakes, you're not trying hard enough."</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11833406562033512660">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_576498066">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/576498066" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/16471876-liz-barnsley" class="left imgcol" title="Liz Barnsley"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1447272680p2/16471876.jpg" alt="Liz Barnsley" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/576498066?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 02, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/16471876-liz-barnsley" class="user" title="Liz Barnsley">Liz Barnsley</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/107379.The_Well_of_Lost_Plots" title="Paperback 9780340825938" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer576498066">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17710371452047672880">This, the 3rd in the "Thursday Next" series of books, finds Thursday taking refuge in an unpublished novel of dubious merit while she ponders her next move in her quest to retrieve her Husband, Landon, cruelly eradicated from history during "Lost in a Good Book". At the same time continuing her training with Jurisfiction and trying desperately to hold onto her memories of Landen and keep them safe from Aornis Hades, as usual she finds herself in all sorts of hilarious trouble..with Miss Haversha</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17710371452047672880">This, the 3rd in the "Thursday Next" series of books, finds Thursday taking refuge in an unpublished novel of dubious merit while she ponders her next move in her quest to retrieve her Husband, Landon, cruelly eradicated from history during "Lost in a Good Book". At the same time continuing her training with Jurisfiction and trying desperately to hold onto her memories of Landen and keep them safe from Aornis Hades, as usual she finds herself in all sorts of hilarious trouble..with Miss Haversham along for the ride to try and keep her out of it. These novels are a complete joy to read and unique in their imaginative world building, I would be a fool to try and find the right words to describe the sheer scale of them. Suffice to say, if you havent yet met Thursday Next nip out and buy a copy of "The Eyre Affair" - and if, like me, you are an avid reader and lover of all types of novels, you will very much appreciate what Jasper Fforde has done here - You will laugh, you may even shed a tear, and you will certainly turn the last page with a wistful sigh and wonder how quickly you can pick up the next story. I would also highly recommend that you pick these books up in print format, not Kindle format, because the cover art and the "tactile" feel of them is another reason that they are so great. In these days of ebooks (and don't get me wrong, I adore my Kindle and would not be without it!) the quest to find great artistry and eye catching covers seems to be losing pace with publishers - and that would be a shame. Enjoy! </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17710371452047672880">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/490228789?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 10, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/715626-joe" class="user" title="Joe">Joe</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer490228789">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15856032015952112245">Well, i didn't guess where Fforde was going to take this one. I figured after going further into the Bookworld with the "Lost in a Good Book' that he would spend more time in the real world this time around. Man was i wrong! "The Well of Lost Plots" took place almost entirely in Bookworld with only brief forays into the outside "reality."<br /><br />This was mostly fun. It felt for most of the book like being inside the literary equivalent of Toon Town from "Who Framed Roger Rabbit?" Fantastic references to</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15856032015952112245">Well, i didn't guess where Fforde was going to take this one. I figured after going further into the Bookworld with the "Lost in a Good Book' that he would spend more time in the real world this time around. Man was i wrong! "The Well of Lost Plots" took place almost entirely in Bookworld with only brief forays into the outside "reality."<br /><br />This was mostly fun. It felt for most of the book like being inside the literary equivalent of Toon Town from "Who Framed Roger Rabbit?" Fantastic references to all your favorite classic (and not so classic) characters abound. Clearly Fforde couldn't get the rights to "Archer and Spade' from the Maltese Falcon so he had to use Parker and Snell.<br /><br />Unfortunately, there just didn't seem to be enough high stakes this time around. The series has a nice balance of serious and funny, but you know it's always good to turn out mostly good at this point. That being said, I love Thursday Next and enjoy discovering Bookworld along with her.<br /><br />One nerdy complaint: No time travel! I demand more time travel!<br /><br />This isn't Shakespeare (except for sometimes when it literally is) but for a quick, fun adventure with hilarious literary references you could do a lot worse. <br /><br />Some great quotes:<br />"If this book is to survive, we have to control the emotions within it; as it is, the novel is three times more barbaric than when first penned - left to its own devices it won't be long before murder and mayhem start to take over completely - remember what happened to that once gentle comedy of errors Titus Andronicus?"<br /><br />He and Randolph were drinking some beers in the kitchen and talking about the correct time to use a long dash to designate interrupted speech.<br />"You can use it any---"<br />"Arnie, I owe you an apology."</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15856032015952112245">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

The Shots aim to promote the short story as well as the work of some Picador\'s greatest authors. They will be contemporarily packaged but ultimately disposable books that are the ideal literary alternative to a magazine.\n\nBret Easton Ellis\' two short stories chronicle the lives of a group of Los Angele\'s residents all of them suffering from nothing less that death of the soul. Ellis has immense gift for dialogue, off-the-wall humour, merciless description and exotic bleakness.\n\nIn \'Water from the Sun\', Cheryl Lane is going under. Her marriage to William has broken down, she has moved in with a young boy half her age who is more interested in other young boys that in her and she keeps not turning up at work, the one area of her life that seems to be in good working order. To keep afloat she drinks, she shops and she takes pills. Would meeting up with William, something she has been avoiding like everything else in her life, give her what she needs anyway?\n\nIn \'Discovering Japan\', Bryan, is on tour.
